ity is the Hon. Charles L. Patton. (Cheers

"I am willing:, gentlemen, to compare
this cenventlon-r-the Republican' conven
tion of San Francisco â and the Republi
can party of San Francisco to the Demo
cratic convention and ' the Democratic
party of Sin Francisco. In a great speech
at Metropolitan Temple a week ago Sat
urday night General Barries . compared
the Republican principles to those of the
fusion party. What is the Democracy of
this municipality but part and portion of
tne party known as the fusion party of
Sacramento? Can any good belong to a
party having affiliated in the unholy
union now existing between the Demo
cratic and Populist parties of the State?
It. is said that parties should nominate
good men. True, but we must look like
wise to principles. It Is as unsafe to nom
inate good men, pledged to support bad
principles, as it is to nominate, dishonest
men to abuse good principles. The gen
tleman whose name I have offered for
your consideration and whom, you will, I
believe, accept as leader of our party in
this municipality, la a man among men.
He is a man who represents Republican
principles. He is a . man loyal to the
principles of the Republican party. He is
loyal to the grand old party, the savior of
our country. He is loyal to our party,
which has preserved our institutions, the
institutions upon which our' Government
was founded; loyal to the man who has
guided the ship of state though the break
ers of strife and war. He is known
throughout the land for his' many noble
characteristics. He holds the respect of
this convention, of the business commu
nity, and is honored tor his executive abil
ity, his courage, his independence, his in
tegrity.
"Mr. Patton is a man who would not
seek office unless he could do so inde
pendently and know that none of hon
esty's principles would have to be sacri
ficed. Under no circumstances would be
attach himself to any element or faction
WhOM principles were inconsistent with
the principles of his party. He is \ Re
publican, has been a Republican and will
be a Republican. He will not affiliate
SAN FRANCISCO, THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 29, 1898.
with party or faction un-Republican or
advocating doctrines inconsistent with its
time-honored principles. I now offer for
your consideration the name of the next

Charles L. Pat-tan."

Nominations for Superior Judges werÂ«

"Mr. Chairman and gentlemen of the
convention: We have now arrived at the
most Important part of the work that will
devolve upon this convention. The people
of the city will scrutinize the men whom
you put forth as your candidates for the
Judges of the Superior Court. I desire to
name a man whom we all love and re
spect. He is James M. Troutt. He has
had a long and an honorable political life
and his elections heretofore on different
occasions have been decisive ones. On
the bench he has proved to be an honor
able man and his decisions have been just
ones, that have been rarely reversed. He
is an affable and genial gentleman, and is
assured of election if nominated."
Delegate Gunzendorfer seconded the
nomination. He was followed by George
Smith and S. Rosenheim, who spoke in
the same strain. Judge Troutt was nom
inated by acclamation, and then to make
it legal the secretary was instructed to
cast- the ballot.
Delegate Caldwell of the Thirty-ninth
District nominated Davis JLouderback. He
said:
"I do not desire to take up your time
by a Lengthy speech as the man 1 name
needs no encomium at my hands. He is
fitted by nature for the honorable position
of Judge.. He is manly and courageous
and has proved himself the possessor of
these qualities in times that have tried
men's souls. He ' has had wide
experience and brings to the posi

the bench. The people owe him a debt of

He is. honest and decent and is a man
who dares to do right at all times. I say
to you nominate Davis Louderback and
so sure as the sunbeams kiss the sky
to-morrow all over our beautiful land
just so sure will he be elected in Novem
ber."

MeCorab.

Edgar D. Peixotto nominated Henry

and the short term H. N. Clement. In

much in his behalf. He is a scholarly
man of marked legal attainments. Ho
helped frame the new charter for this
city, and his work is apparent through
out that instrument. In the election for
Freeholder he led almost all the other
candidates. If he is elected he will add
luster and pride to the party. He has
all the requirements needed of a man
holding a judicial position. I say that
to sit in judgment on your fellow-man
is as near the solemnity of the after
world as anything can be."

Not Pledged to De Young.
The assertion made recently by the Ex
aminer- that the legislative candidates on
the Republican ticket In Alameda County
are pledged to vote, in the event of their
election, for M. H. de Young for United
States Senator, is a falsehood made out
of whoie cloth. . The legislative nominees
are not pledged for De Young or for any
other man. and Injustice to them It is
only fair to make the iact known to the
public. â¢

THE DEMOCRATIC SLATE
He Arraigned the Managers of the Municipal
Convention Last Night and Was
Wildly Cheered.
â¢ . . . â 
The Programme for Coroner Failed, but the Candidates for
Police Judges and Justices of the Peace Went
Through Without a Hitch.
Ex-Senator Bart Burke of Santa Cruz
raised his voice in angry protest last
night against the manner in which the
"slate" was being run through in the
Democratic .municipal convention. The
gallery and audience were with him and
cheered until their throats gave out.
Burkes arraignment of the managers
of the convention upset part of their pro
gramme. Dr. E. E. Hill, an assistant
surgeon at the water front branch of the
Receiving Hospital, who was not on the
"slate," carried off the nomination for
Coroner much to the disgust of the self
appointed bosses-. They had promised to
give the nomination to Dr. George M.
Terrill, but after Burkes speech they
were unable to carry out their contract.
The convention was called to order In
Odd Fellows' Hall at 8:1.". o'clock by
Chairman McGee, who announced that
the first order of business would ba the
report of the committee appointed to
canvass the names of aspirants for nomi
nation for the School Board.
â  Ex-Judge Cope, chairman of the com
mittee, reported that while many names
had been canvassed, no selections or rec
ommendations had been agreed upon. He
promised, however, to have the report
ready, to-night when the convention meets
in B'nai B'rith Hall.
George McGillivray reported for the
committee on Supervisors that they
would not be able to report before to
night, and further time was granted.
.timothy Lyons then placed Michael
Me.ipher, whom he described as an argo
naut of 1832, In nomination for Recorder.
"Mprigher is an oN.-time Democrat," said
Lyons, "and as he is out of business now
he will "have leisure and time to devote
to the office."
Edmund Godchaux. secretary of the

cordership by Sidney M. Van Wyck Jr.
Van "Wyck asked the delegates to do
their duty by nominating Godchaux, not
withstanding any combinations that
might have been made. "When God
chaux was in. the Legislature," Bald Van
Wyck, "his every vote showed that he
was opposed to the railroad, and that is
the kind of men we want on our ticket."
Barna McKinne started out to second
the nomination of Godchaux and wound
up. with a denunciation of the railroad
and its methods. "I want to know," said
he, "if the railroad can come in here and
terrify the delegates? It Is a bad busi
ness to have the railroad running things."
McKinne then sld-etracked and was pro
ceeding, to name prominent Democratic
attorneys who were retained by the rail
road to fight damage suits, when some
one in tho gallery called time and he sat
down. : â¢ . . â  . .
Max Popper, in seconding the. nomina
tion of Moagher, said Meagher was one
of. the old-time' Democrats, and that he
had almost taken his life in hia hands
when he marched in Democratic proces
sions in 1860.
Judge Cope pleaded with the conven
tion to "do the fair thing" by nominating:
Godchaux/ He pleaded in vain, however,
for Meagher was on the "slate," having
been chosen by the bosses instead of ex-
Senator Plnder, who was driven out of
the fight by the Examiner.
The roll was called and Meagher receiy-
Ed 49 votes. Oodchaux had thirty-eight
friends In the convention, who voted for
him.
The nomination of Meagher was then
made unanimous on motion of Van Wyck.

AGAINST FUSION
IN ANY FORM.
The State Central Committee of the National Democratic party, at its
meeting held in this city on Tuesday fevening, September 27, unanimously
passed the following resolution:
This committee calls attention to the further abandonment of Demo
cratic principles b- the fusion with un-Democratic and anti-Democratic ele
ments of this State, and strongly advises all members of the National
Democratic party to aid in such way as may seen most effective in the
election of sound money representatives to Congress and in the election of
a Legislature which will choose a United States Senator, who will support
sound, money and safe financial legislation.
k WILLIAM THOMAS, chairman
ELLIOTT MCALLISTER, secretary,

HAD TO ABANDON HER
BIG LUMBER RAFT
THE PBOGBESO FOBCED FROM
THE CONTEST.
Stuck to Her Tow for Eleven Days
and Then Had to Cut Adrift
When Off Point Beyes.
The steamer Progreso arrived in port
yesterday without the raft of piles she
was towing down. Heavy weather was
encountered, and one-half of the .huge
mass broke adrift. Early yesterday morn
ing a heavy swell and a nasty cross sea
was met and the. rest of the raft broke
adrift. When the Progreso reached port
all she had to show for eleven days of
hard towing was about. sixty torts of chain
which was towing behind her.
As the Progreso gave up the attempt
j to bring the raft into port, the Humboldt
j is going to try it. She got in from Seat
tle yesterday at 2 p. m., and . an hour
later had discharged her passengers and
was on her way out to sea to look for the.
derelict. Captain Bonnlfleld had an ex
perience with the menace to navigation
early in the morning. At 8 a. m. the
i lirst portion was met, end from that hour
â  up to 10:30 a. m. floating piles were con
stantly passed. A lookout was then kept
for the remainder of the raft, and it-'wus
! sighted six or seven miles west of Point
i Reyes. " Seas were breaking clear over it,
I but it was intact. Should the Humboldt
| bring the remains of the raft in it will
be a lucky find for that fortunate vessel.
The Alaska Commercial Company's
i steamer Bertha arrived frqm St. Michael
yesterday. She brought down about sixty
miners and nearly a million' in gold, dust
I and drafts. Fourteen boxes for the Ala's-
I ka Commercial Company contained $350,000
! worth cf dust alone. The steamer Cleve
land was still nt St. Michael when I . the
Bertha sailed. Pat Galvin, who owns the
steamer's cargo, had to purchase the river
I steamer Tukoner in order to get his stuff
up to Dawson. .As soon as -it has been
discharged from' the Cleveland into the
Yukoner the former will return to San
Francisco.
T. P. H. Whltelaw and the workmen
who went Up with him era the Slntram
will return on the next trip of the Leele
naw. They have completed all the rivjÂ»r
boats and barges save one, and that one
will remain on the stocks until next
spring.
There was a thrilling accident on the
British ship Alcinous yesterday. The tug
Sea King was taking her to sea, and when
two miles outside the heads a sailor fell
overb oard from the ship's forecastle. Cap
tain Harry Marshall, of the Sea King,
saw the man fall and slowed up. The
men on the ship saw the accident also, for
as the sailor drifted past the vessel's
stern a line was thrown and caught, and
in a few seconds the sailor was aboard
again, none the worse for his ducking.
It was a wonderfully smart piece of work.
DOCKERY'S TRIAL
WAS POSTPONED
THE WITNESSES FOR THE PROS
ECUTION WERE MISSING.
Continuance Granted to October 19 So
as to Secure Their Attendance
if j-'ossible.
The Board of Health did not try Chief
Food Inspector Dockery last evening. The
Inspector was rendy and so was the
board, but the complainant, Isaac Leip
sic, scored a dismal failure as a prosecut
ing quantity. He did, in fact, appear be
fore the Board, being represemed by At
torney Wilson, but was only able to an
nounce that his witnesses, some twenty
in number, were all missing. Mr. Wilson
stated that he had just been called into
the case and knew little of its merits. He
asked whether the Board of Health had
power to subpoena witnesses to the case,
admitting the inability of his client to
get those. he had counted upon without
the compulsory process of the law. He
declared t hut Mr. Leipsic was confident
or proving that "Inspector Dockery was
incompetent, showed favoritism and was
moreover, addicted to drunkenness:"
Attorney Dunn of the. Prosecuting At
torney s office, representing Dockerv
stated that his side , was ready and
anxious to go ahead: that twenty-three
witnesses were present to vouch for Mr.
Dockery and rebut all charges brought
against him. The Inspector wanted no
whitewash and would do all in his power
to assist Mr. Leipsic in getting the case
tried.
Wilson replied that several of the com
plainant s witnesses had been present ear
lier in the evening, but had gone away.
He would be obliged to ask for a post
ponement arid any assistance that the
board could give in securing the attend
ance of witnesses for the prosecution.
Dunn assured his opponent that any
nelp which the prosecuting attorney's of
fice could rive in corraling the absent
witnesses for the other side would be
freely extended. At the same time he
stated that so. far as he knew the Board
of Health had no power to compel the
attendance of witnesses.
It. was finally determined to allow Mr
Llepsic to have another chance at Dock
ery on Wednesday, October 19.
The board then settled down to the con
sideration of Texas fever among Solano
County cattle and to devise some method
or keeping the diseased meat out of the
Â°Â«n Francisco market. Dr. Bazet stated
that the epidemic was becoming wide
spread in Solano County and certain
other sections of the State, and that some
Pteps should be taken to regulate the sale
of cattle dying from Texas fever.
Dr. Root presented evidence to the
board that the fever was now developing
all over the State, and declared that the
meat of cattle dying from it was being
shipped to this city. He further said that
there were so few outward symptoms of
disease in the infected cattle "that an in
spection by ordinary laymen was almost
useless. As for the meat being unhealthy'
to consume, he thought that it was far
preferable to other diseased meat, but
still was objectionable on account of the
febrile conditions of the cattle preceding
death.
The whole matter was finally referred
to the State Board of Health: "

HEAVYWEIGHT PUGILISTS.
JACK STELZNER and JOE KENNEDY. Who Will Meet To-Morrow Night
... in Woodward's Pavilion,
Joe Kennedy, the popular heavy-weight pugilist of the Excelsior Ath
letic Club, who has trained down from 220 pounds to 190 pounds in six
weeks by a Course of hard training, will meet the clever and hard-hitting
Jack Stelzner in a twenty-round contest to-morrow evening at Woodward's
Pavilion.
Kennedy was always selected by Tom Sharkey whenever the pugilistic
sailor had use for a boxing paTtner in this city. Sharkey frequently said
that Kennedy, when In condition, should hold his own with any heavy
weight In the land. In fact, the sailor had a great respect for Kennedy's
right fist, which on more than one occasion stirred up the fighting Dlood of
the herculean Irishman during sparring sessions.
Stel*ner, who has been the sparring partner of Champion Bob Fitzsim
mons, will introduce many new moves, which will doubtless puzzle the
representative of the Excelsior Club, whose style of fighting is round
arm. Contests between heavy-weights are always pleasing to the sport
ing; fraternity of this city, who delight to hear the sounds of the big
gloves when they patter on body or face.
The preliminaries will consist of ten-round contests between Patsy
Corrlgan and Jack La Rue, and Phil Green and Young Peter Jackson.
STRUCK THE BOY
WHO BIT HIS HAND
COLONEL PARNELL CHARGED
WITH CRUELTY.
The Secretary of the Society for the
Prevention of Cruelty to Chil
dren Explains.
Colonel Parnell, secretary of the Cali
fornia Society for the Prevention of Cru
elty to Children, has aroused the Indig
nation of a number of occupants of the
Central Market building at 223 Powell
street through his alleged, cruelty to a
nine-year-old boy who was left In his
custody by a mother who could not sup
port her children.
The charge has been placed in the hands
of Secretary Wadham of the Eureka So
ciety for the Protection of Children, and
he is now gathering evidence with a view
to having Colonel Parnell punished for
cruelty.
The trouble grew out of Mrs. C, John
son's effort to have her son fiarl and her
daughter Valentine sent to a charitable
institution, as she was unable to support
them. Young Johnsqn was brought to the
society's room on .Friday last, and when
the mother started to leave Without him.
he set up nn â  unearthly creaming-. .The
occupants of neighboring offices ran Into
the hall to learn the cause and- discovered
that they emanated from the society's
headquarters. The . doors were found
locked, and in trying to gain admittance
they attracted the attention of Mrs. Pres
cott, the assistant secretary, who opened
the door. At that woment the colonel wad
seen to slap the boy. and was immediate
ly remonstrated with by Indignant witr
nesses. . â 
A Dr. Henderson attempted to enter the
colonel's presence witn a view to stopping
further punishment, -when 'he was firmly
and coldly told- to leave. On his refusal
to- do so the now indignant secretary
pushed him out and closed and locked the
door.
One lady, Mrs. Sherman by name, who
has adjoining offices, was so grieved at
the boys treatment that she cried all af-'
ternoon. In speaking about the incident
yesterday, she said:- "I thought it was
strange that a society that cares â -. for
children should- treat them so Inhumanly
as did Colonel Parnell. its representative.
I was shocked, and had I been a man he
would not have acted as he did."
Secretary' Parnell's version of the affair
places the case in an entirely different
light. He stated that the boy- became
hysterical as soon as the. mother left
him. He screamed and carried on in a,
manner that caused -nearly â " all of the
people in the building to assemble In the
hall. Mrs. Prescott did all that ' she
could to quiet him, but the youngster

SLEEK INSURGENTS INDIFFERENT
STARVATION STALKS
IN MATANZAS STREETS
Special Cable to The Call ud the New York Herald. Copy rich ted, IS9B, by Jamej Gordon Bennett.
MATAXZAS, Cuba, Sept. 28. â Matanzas, once rich and: prosperous, to-day presents a sad picture of con
tradiction. Here one sees the streets "filled with starving wretches who die almost too fast to find men to, bury
them. Twenty- rive thousand reconcentrados began congregating in the city in January of last year. A month
later they began to die from hunger, and have continued doing so ever since. Between January, 1897, and Sep
tember, 1898. 10,066 persons died in the city of Matanzas. 80 per cent of them, according to the doctors' certifi
cates, from starvation and nothing else. Fully half the reconcentrados have no shelter at night. They live and
sleep in the streets. It is almost impossible to walk a block in the heart of the city without seeing some wreck
of a human beihg. too weak to go farther, lying pn the sidewalk, gasping and moaning. Mothers, half skeletons
themselves, crouch with sad-faced children under the shadow of the arches at the palace, begging centavos of the
soldiers, who drive them away. Five or six drop dead in the streets every day. The first duty of the "Guardia Civ
iles" in making their rounds in the streets each morning is to look for the bodies of persons who died duringthe
nigh* . â¢ . â¢ . . :' .â¢ â¢â¢ .
NOTED JURIST
AND DIPLOMAT
PASSES AWAY
Thomas F. Bayard Dies
at His Daughter's
Residence.
DISSOLUTION VERY GRADUAL
Career of tfie Statesman Who
Senred as Secretary of State
and Embassador.
Bpecial Dispatch to The Call.
DEDHAM, Mass.. Sept. 28.â Thomas
F. Bayard died at half-past 4 o'clock
this afternoon at Karlsteih, the sum
mer residence of his daughter, Mrs.
Samuel D. Warren, after an illness of
cix weeks. His death was without
pain. His wife, his two daughters,
Mrs. Warren and Miss Florence Bay
ard, and his son. Thomas F. Jr., saw
him draw his last breath, and his
third daughter, the Countess Lauen
haupt, was on her wav to Dedham.
The remains will be conveyed td
Delaware and the formal services will
be held Saturday in the old Swedish
church at Wilmington.
Karlstein lies off the Xeedham road.
Here about the middle of August came
Mr. Bayard, to be the guest of his
daughter and his son-in-law. He was
sick, but it was given out that his ill
ness was merely due to declining years.
]Ip steadily grew vorse, however, and
had spells of semi-consciousness arid of
delirium. A consultation of physicians
â¢was held August 26, and they agreed
that Mr. Bayard was suffering, from
artuns colorosls and a general break-
Ing down Incident to age. He suf
fered no pain t" any noticeable degree,
ar.d the chief tendency was to sleep.
During the first few weekp of his ill
ness Mr. Bayard was able to sit up.
bat as the days passed he became
gradual^' weaker, and three weeks ago
he laid, himself on his bed and never
again rose from it. At times he would
rally to Home extent, but the relapse
carried him always nearer the end. His
â wonderful constitution resisted the
ravages of disease for a surprising
period. Much of the time he was In a
Bemi-conscious condition, seldom rec
'srnizing any of the family and at in
tervals h-ving sinking spells, accom
panied by choking. These left him al
ways weaker. Yesterday it became
evident that his death was a, matter of
only a few hours. Still he lingered,
having a slight rally during the night
and during the forenoon his condition
showed little change. At 4 o'clock he
V-nn to Pink rapiCly. and half an
hour later he gently passed away. To
morrow the body will be sent to Dela
ware, being due" to arrive at Wilming
ton late Friday afternoon.
JURIST AND DIPLOMAT.
Career of Thomas Francis Bayard in
Which He Gained Distinction.
Thomas Francis Bayard was a distin
guished member of an eminent family.
Since long before the revolutionary war
times the Bayards have boen conspicuous
In the country's history as patriots and
statesmen, and many, acqilred national
fame as Jurists. Four mc-rnbers of the
Bayard family have had Senatorial hon
ors bestowed upon them by the / little
Btate of Delawareâ Thomas Francis Bay
ard, his father. James Asheton Bayard:
his grandfather, of the same name, the
negotiator of the treaty of Ghent, and his
uncle, Richard Henry Bayard. HA grand
mother's father. Governor Bassett of Del
aware, was also the recipient of Senator
ial honors.
Thomas Francis Bayard was born in
Wilmington, Del., October 29. 182 S. and was
a younger son. In his thirteenth year he
entered school at Flushing, L. I. Here
he remained two years. At the expim
tion of that period, being intended by his
father for mercantile pursuits, a clerkship
was obtained for him in the establishment
of Mr. Bayard's brother-in-law, August
Van Cortlandt Schermerhorn. Here he re
ceived an excellent business training. In
1848 his elder brother died, whereupon, at
the request of his parents, he returned to
Wilmington. Having a liking for his
father's profeesfon, that of law, he be
came a student with that end In view,
THOMAS FRANCIS BAYARD.
and was. admitted to the bar In ISSI. He
immediately began the practice of his pro
fession with his father, and was success
ful from the beginning, his local fame
spreading rapidly. In 1858 he was ap
pointed United States District Attorney
for Delaware, but resigned the following.
year. â¢ â¢ '
. When the war of thÂ« rebellion began
Mr. Bayard was pursuing his profession.
With the first muuerings of war the
people of Wilmington set about estab
fishing means of, .self-protection. A mili
tary company was organized and Thomas
F. Bayard was elected its first lieuten
ant. In June, ISCO, the famous peace
meeting of citizens- was held at Dover,
and Lieutenant Bayard was. one of the
principal speakers. He denounced the
war, and his remarks on that occasion
have been quoted in later years as an
argument against his availability as a
Presidential candidate.
Meanwhile Mr. Bayard's popularity In
his native State kept growing 1 rapidly,
and In ISGS he was elected to succeed
his father in the United States Senate.
It Is noteworthy that on the day he was
elected to the Senate, for the. full term,
his father, who had resigned, ..was also
re-elected a Senator from Delaware to
serve for the unexplred part of his orig
inal term. This is the only case of a
father and san being voted for by the
same Legislature to fill the -Senatorial
offices.
In October. 18T.6, Senator Bayard was
married to Louisa, daughter of Joseph
Lee, a Baltimore banker. Twelve chil
dren were the result of the union. In
1877 Senator Bayard received the hon-
orary degree of LL.D. from Harvard
College. ; â  (. " '
' As soon" as the result of the national
election of 1884 was positively known Mr.
Bayard was the first Democratic states
man- invited to. consult . with President
elect Cleveland, and it was generally un
derstood that he was the first man of
fered' a place In the new Cabinet â the
leading place at that. This he finally ac
cepted, although It was said that he was
'strongly advised by leading Democrats
not to enter the Cabinet at all. asjiis de
parture from the Senate . would . tend to
weaken the strength of the party in' that
body. â¢ '."â ".â¢;:
At the close of Mr. Cleveland's admin
istration Mr. Bay. returned to private
life and to his legal profession. /
In March, 1898, Mr. Bayard' was appoint
ed Embassador to the Court of St. James
and served during Mr. Cleveland's second
term. :
. Mr. Bayard's first wife died In the first
year of his term as Secretary, of State.
Four years later he was married to Miss
Mary W. Cllnmar of Washington, who
survives him." Seven of the children' are
living, as follows: Mrs. Samuel D: War
ren of Boston. Miss Annie Bayard, Miss
Florence Bayard,- the Countess . Lauen
haupt, formerly Miss Nellie Bayard;
James A. Bayard'/ Thomas F. Bayard Jr.
and Philip E. Bayard. >,
STRONG TESTIMONY
. AGAINST MMNTYRE
The Prosecution Announces That It
Will Close Its Case To-.tforrow \
Morning.
DENVER, Sept. 28.â The court-martial"
of Chaplain Joseph P. Mc.Tntyre of the :
battleship Oregon was resumed this
morning. Several witnesses testified that
the chaplain's remarks had been correctly
reported in the newspapers'. The sub
stance .of their testimony was that Chap
lain Mclntyre charged Admiral Sampson
with reporting himself within four miles
of the Spanish ship .Colon when she sur
rendered, when In reality he was at least
eight miles away; that he charged Cap
tain Evans of the lowa with running
away from the fight, referring to him
contemptuously as "Fighting Bob. " and
that he charged that Eastern shipbuild
ers obtained contracts from the Govern- !
ment by means of political pulls, and
That the Oregon was the only battleship
that was honestly built.
After these witnesses had testified
Judge Advocate Lauchefmer gave notice
that the prosecution would rest its casÂ»
to-morrow morning and would probably
introduce no more witnesses.

VANDALS ROBBED
COLUMBUS' TOMB
Portion of the Remains
Missing.
Officials Observe Great Secrecy in the
Farcical Ceremony of Remov
ing the Explorer's
Bones.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
HAVANA, Sept. 28 (via Key "West,
Fla., Sept. 2Sr.)â Mystery and secrecy
surround all the official acts of the
Spanish Government. Especially was :
this the case In exhuming the remains
of Columbus, and this fact has caused
much unfavorable- comment and given
rise to errave rumors, which, though un
confirmed, appf lr to have some found
ation, as there has been no attempt at
denial from any source. The. removal
of the remains, which should have
been- a public ceremony, was' veiled in"!
the deepest mystery and was effected
behind closed doors of the: cathedral,
which was guarded by an armed force.'
Few of the authorities were present,
the names of those who were to attend
being handed. to the Chief of Police the |
night before in General Blanco's own
handwriting. The- Chief of PollceÂ«sug
gested that comments would be made
by the press upon the secrecy of the
proceedings and asked whether it would
not be advisable to allow representar
tives. of the newspapers to be present.
To this General Blanco positively re- .
fused to accede, telling the Chief of Po
lice to limit himself exclusively to the
list of guests which had been handed
to hi.m.
" No official account of the ceremony
or statement regarding the condition
i of the remains' has been made public,
â¢ but it has leaked out from an almost
authoritative source that they are not
in exactly the same condition as when
they were placed in the niche. When
\ the remains were placed in the maiiso
l leum a small casket was enclosed, con
taining coins, gold and silver medal's,
preckms stones and rolls of parchment,
as was then, the custom upon the occa
sion of a burial. It appears that this
j casket had been tampered with and
i rifled of its contents, and further ru
mors say that only portions of the re
mains arr> left.
Gossip of scandal and speculation ere
rife, the truth of which the authori
ties make.no effort to affirm or contra
dict. A quasi-official explanation,.
1 however, is to the effect that secrecy
was given to the ceremony because a
cable dispatch received here reported
that General Toral had been murdered
in Madrid the day before, and fears
were entertained that disorders might
, occur if the public and military came
together in great numbers.
Some of the more radical Spaniards
!go to the length of saying that the
whole ceremony was a farce, the Gov
ernment at Washington having object
ed to the removal of the remains and
this Government going through panto
mime in order -to make the public be
; Meve the remains, which are really left
I here, were shipped to Spain.-
CUBAN SOLDIERS FEAST
WHILE NATIVES PERISH
Special Cable to The Call and the New York Herald. C opyrlgrh ted, 1898, by James Gordon Bennett.
â Â® MATANZ AS, Cuba, Sept. 28. â About four miles from the city is the camp of General Betancourt, the in-
O surgent commander of Matanzas province. I visited it to-day with General Betancourt. There are about 700
Â® men, all well clothed and well fed. General Betancourt led me himself into the hut used for commissary stores.
O It was piled high with barrels of beans, cornmeal and. jerked beef. General Betancourt told me his men were
0 regularly supplied .with all the food they wanted by systematic contributi6ns from rich Cuban sympathizers. Ma
Â© tanzas thus has plenty of food for able-bodied men, but hardly a loaf of bread for the women and children dying
0 like dogs, in the streets. In the course of an interview I had with General Betancourt, he said:
O "We began this revolution against Spain for our independence,, nothing else. The thought of annexation
0 never entered our minds. We will never lay down our arms until Cuba is absolutely independent. It is all non
q sense for the United States to send any soldiers or officers here. We are abundantly able to take care of that
0 part of it ourselves." . :
QUEEN LOUISE
OF DENMARK IS
CALLED TO REST
Death of the Aged Wife of King
Christian IX Early This
Morning.
Was Known as the Mother-in-Law of Europe
and the Counselor of Crowned
Heads.
Special Dispatch to The Ca'.l.
COPENHAGEN, Sept. 29.â The Queen
of Denmark died at 5:40 this morning.
Queen Louise was born September 7,
loi7, and was the daughter of Land
grave Wilhelm of Hesse-Cassel. She
was married to King Christian IX of
Denmark May 26, 1842.
l^ast August the report was received
that she had lost her mind and had
suddenly collapsed into, a state of
dotage.
Her illness was mental rather than
physical, and It was a great grief to
Queen Louise's family- and entourage,
for she was known until recently as
one of- the most clever women in the
Old World. She has been nicknamed
the "Mother-in-law of Europe," and it
is certain that throughout the reign of
the late Emperor Alexander of Russia
she. was his source, of inspiration and
his personal counselor, besides acting
as the chief adviser of her favorite son,
who reigns as King George of Greece.

AS TO ANNEXATION
Republicans Deem It Necessary to

NEW YORK, Sept. 28.â A Washington special to the Herald 6ays:
New York Republicans have struck a keynote an the subject' of Phil
ippine annexation, which will probably be taken up by their party
throughout the country, and by the time the Congressional campaign
ia fairly under way the party will be thoroughly committed to an
nexation. At the Republican Congressional headquarters the greatest
importance, is attached to the necessity of electing a Republican
House of Representatives this fall, on account of the effect it will
have upon European nations, by showing them that the President has
the support of the country, in his policy in dealing with the territory
conquered from Spain. Representative Hull of lowa, chairman of the
Republican Executive Committee, said to-day: "Europe would regard
the election of a Democratic .House of Representatives as a vote of
lack of confidence in the administration and its policy, and would take
advantage of the situation to attempt to keep this country from shar
ing in the benefits that are to follow as a result of the opening up of
the Orient to- commerce."

WILL CONDUCT
INVESTIGATION
AT SANTIAGO
Commission Will Gall Upon Gen
erals Miles and Shafter
to Testify.
Commander in Chief of trje Arroy May Decline,
Preferring to Trust His Case
to Congress.
NEW YORK, Sept. 28.â The "Wash-
ington correspondent of the Herald tel
egraphs: After completing their labors
In Washington the commissioners who
are investigating the conduct of the
war will go to Santiago. This will be
the last thing done. They expect to
spend a week in the Cuban port. An
examination will be made of the point
of landing of the army and of the facil
ities for landing supplies. Every point
bearing upon the work before the com
mission will be covered. This will in
volve evidence from both General Miles
and General Shafter.
Both General Miles and General
Shafter will be asked to go upon the
stand. They may refuse, it Is thought
by. some members of the commission,
but this Is not considered probable. The
commission, however, will spare no ef
fort to get a hearing from them. No
doubt would have been entertained as
to the willingness of these generals to
testify but for one fact. General MiTes
recently called upon Chairman Dodge
of the commission and requested that
he be not called befere it. General
Dodge gave no satisfactory answer,
but the understanding of the commis
sion is that General Miles had made up
his mind to be satisfied with nothirg
less than a Congressional Investigation.
From another source 1 learn that
General Mites has his version of the
Santiago campaign fully formulated
and when an investigation is had he
wishes to have It rigorous, acute and
final.
There is no concealment of the fact
that there are many differences between
General Miles and General Shafter, and
General Miles prefers to trust his cas>?
to Congress. In spite of this he will
be asked to appear before the commis
sion.
Secretary Alger also will certainly
bt asked to appear.
WASHINGTON, Sept. 28.â Only four
members of the commission appointed
to investigate the conduct of. the war
were present at the forenoon session
to-day, and In the absence of replies to
the interrogatories sent to the Secre
tary of War and his subordinates very
little business was transacted. Colonel
Denby was . elected vice-chairman of
the commission, and In the absence of
Chairman Dodge presided over the
session to-day.
The only Important matter brought
to the attention of the commission was
a report by General Wilson of the en
gineer department of the army, of
which he is the chief. No statement
was called for from him because there
were no charges against this depart
ment, but General Wilson, being a
member of the commission, concluded
that to furnish one would be an appro
priate proceeding. The document was
held for disposition, by the full com
mission, and, consequently, was not
given to the press. It is a detailed
statement showing the condition of the
department on the Ist of last April.
Just before the declaration of war. as
compared with Its condition on the 31st
of August last, after the cessation of
hostilities, and it deals especially with
torpedoes and fortifications. Included
in it is a letter written by General Wil
son to his subordinates throughout the
country just before the beginning of
the war, advising them of the prospect
for hostilities and directing what
course they should pursue as mem
bers of the engineer department.
A few letters were received from
persons offering to come before the
commission. These were replied to
with a circular letter, of which the fol
lowing is a copy:
Dear Sir: Referring to your communi
cation of the commission requests
that on receipt of this you will forward
a written statement, giving in as specific
and complete a form as may be practic
able all the facts, based upon your own
personal experience and knowledge, that
will aid the pending investigation. This
statement will be for the information of
the commission only.
SECRETARY OF WAR
COMPLETES HIS TOUR
WASHINGTON, Sept. 28.â Secretary
Alger has returned to Washington. He
came to the War Department imme
diately after breakfast, and was soon
besieged by a number of persons who
had been waiting for his return.
Later he made a verbal report to the
President on the inspection of the army
camps, which he has Just concluded.
During the interview Secretary Long
came over from the Navy Department
and joined the party in the Cabinet
room.
On leaving the White House Secre
tary Alger said he was much gratified
with the conditions of the camps gen
erally. In a general way the camps
were kept satisfactorily, but there
were, he conceded, points which m;ght
have been improved upon, citing par
ticularly the unsatisfactory condition
of the sinks at some places.
"The main trouble found," said Sec
retary Alger, "was, perhaps, the lack
of care the troops took of themselves."
Secretary Alger says the report of a
scene in General Breckinridge's head
quarters at Lexington when he was
inspecting that camp was absolutely
without foundation. There was no
scene, no high words and no excited
language. â  The Secretary says the story
was made out of whole cloth. Secre
tary Alger and Generals Ludington and
Sternberg each have lettens from Gen
eral Wiley, who was reported as hav
ing made accusations against them, ex
pressing his regret that any such report
was circulated.

UNCLE SAM WILL
NOT LOSE ON THE
CENTRAL PACIFIC
Assistant Secretary Vanderlip
Says the Road Is Able to
Pay tfje Debt
He Also Tells of Plans for Shipping Silver
Bullion F ron ) Philadelphia to
San Francisco.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CalJ Office, Riggs House;.
Washington, Sept. 23.
Assistant Secretary Yamlcrlip of the
Treasury Department returned to-night
from a trip to Chicago. He went there
on two missions, one to investigate the
condition of the Central Pacific Rail
road and the other to arrange for an
enormous shipment of silver bullion
across the continent. Mr. Vanderlip
Bald to-night that he had not com
pleted either mission. It was impossi
ble to see many of the people he de
sired to consult about the Central Pa
cific and he had not completed arrange
ments for the shipment of bullion.
"It is contemplated," he said, "to
Ehip about ten million ounces of silver
bullion across the continent from the
Mint in Philadelphia to the Mint in
San Francisco. Such shipments are
usually made by express and we have
a contract %vith the express companies
to ship all the money by express. But
bullion is not money any more than
pig iron is, and there is no more rea
son why we should ship pigs of silver
by express than we should ship pigs
of lead that way. Ten million ounces
of silver weighs 833,333 pounds troy or
625,000 pounds avoirdupois. This is no
trifling weight and it would be a great
expense to the Government to ship it
by express. It will take fifteen or twen
ty cars to carry this amount. If we
ship it by express the companies will
assume the risk and furnish guards,
hut if we ship it by freight we furnish
our own guards and take the risk.
Really I believe there is little risk,
hardly any more so than if it were lead.
We would select the heaviest pigs,
weighing 1000 ounces, or about sixty
five pounds. These would be loaded on
cars and a tourist car placed at each
end of the train.
"The guards would occupy the cars,
and every time the train stopped they
would surround it and prevent any one
from approaching it. There would be
little opportunity to remove any of the
pigs, as the cars would be new and of

NEW YORK, Sept. 28.â The Wash
ington correspondent of the. Herald teU
egraphs: No official information has
been .received, in Washington regarding
the intention of Aguinaldo to formally
inaugurate his so-called republic of. the
Philippines, at Malolos to-morrow and
issue a proclamation to the nations of
the world setting forth the intentions
of his government. It. is not believed,
however, by the officials of the admin
istration that either Admiral Dewey Or
General Otis will take any notice of
the proceedings at Malolos, or that they
will accept the invitations to witness
them. Should Aguinaldo issue his con
templated proclamation to the nations
of the world his action will .have no ef
fect whatever on the peace negotia
tions at Paris. The United States will
certainly not recognize Aguinaldo as
the head of an independent government
and it is not believed that any other
nation will do so. The war is still
technically in progress between Spain
and the United States, and if any third
i power should recognize the insurgents
as independent during the progress, of
the war and during the pendency of
negotiations to determine the disposi
tion of the territory claimed by the in
surgents either the United States or
Spain would have the right to regard
such recognition as a. hostile act. It
would certainly be so regarded by the
United States, and there is no fear here
that any nation in Europe will take a
step which could only be. intended to

PARIS, Sept. 28.â The Gaulois says:
"In spite of the mystery surrounding:
the .matter we are able, from a high
source, to give an outline of the. posi

Americans have instructions which are
much more precise than those of the
Spaniards, there is a diversity of opin
ion in regard to the Philippines. Sen
ator Gray is ' a very ardent Democrat,
and consequently belongs to the party
which repudiates expansion. He op
poses all annexation, and his opinion,
therefore, totally differs from that of
the other four commissioners, some of
whom would be content. with a coaling
station at the Philippines, possibly Ca
vite, while others, like Senator Davis,
advocate the annexation of the whole
archipelago."'
The Gaulois, however, believes that
tne Americans, by mutual concessions,
will come to an agreement, and that
they wiir be largely influenced by ;>su
jor General Merritt's reports on the sit
uation. The instructions to the Span
ish commission, according to the Gau
lois, are, briefly, to hold out and make
the best terms possible. Madrid is well
aware, it appears, that the conferences,
at the best, are only had to please the
Spanish people, and the. only hopes en
tertained are that the Americans will
not abuse the situation too much. Pop
ular sentiment in Spain, it is added,
thinks it. better to abandon the Philip
pine Islands than to keep them under
conditions rendering them ungovern

Special Dispatch to The Call.
RIVERSIDE, Sept. 28.â The tour of
Henry T. Gage and General W. H. L.
Barnes through Southern California
has aroused exceptional enthusiasm
among the rnasies. Popular ovations
which distinguished the speaking at
Santa Barbara, Ventura, San Bernar*
dino, San Diego and Santa Ana are
seldom witnessed in. other than a Pres
idential -campaign-. The ..multitudes
which assemble to hear the standard
bearer and the eloquent exponent of
Republican principles bring with them
Democrats, Populists and so-called Sil
ver Republicans.
Throughout Riverside and Orange
counties the people are aroused to the
dangers o-f Magiiireism and apprehend
that the election of the. fusion ticket
with Maguire at the head would bring
disaster- to' the State, In comparison
with which another dry season would
be but an incident. The fruit growers
and others of the industrial population
In the Santa Ana Valley are looking
forward with confidence, to a period of
great prosperity. Already many mort
gages have been lifted as the result of
toil arid economy, and inquiries con
cerning the Investment of capital for
the future development .of the great
resources of this region impart confi
dence. Men who have wxirked hard
to upbuild the State shudder at the
suggestion of a Muguire triumph and
the introduction of socialistic and sin
gle tax delusions in the affairs of Cali
fornia. The earnestness of the people
is manifested at every great demon
stration.
It is readily observed that Gage is
growing rapidly in popularity. People
hearing Him talk are convinced or his
sincerity. Any man who is sincere can
get a good hearing in these intelligent
and progressive cities of Southern Cali
fornia.
Another strong point in Gage's favor
is that he has never fattened at the
public crib. He never held a politi
cal office and never sought to hold one;
he never drew a dollar from the public
treasury. Industrious men who till the
orchard, run the farms and â¢ pay the
taxes- necessary for the administration
of government view with distrust the
type of politician known as â¢ the
"chronic office seeker" who lives on the
public and is never happy when- he is
not drawing a fat salary from the pub
lic treasury. Gage comes from the
people, and comes free from the taint
of persistent office holding.-
The reception accorded to Henry T.
Gage, W. H. L. Barnes and J. C. Need
ham, the Congressional . nominee, to
day and to-night, attest the enthusiasm
of the people ' of Riverside County.
Coming from the scene of a great out
pouring at Santa Ana, the standard
bearer and General Barnes arrived
shortly before noon at Riverside. At
the railway station they were received
by -a. delegation of leading citizens
headed by C. F. Huse, chairman of the
Republican County Central Committee,
and escorted to the Glenwood Tavern.
Gage Is still among his friends and
neighbors and soon many of them came
forward to grasp his hand. An intro
duction- to .General Barnes is seldom
necessary- If he does not know every
one, every one knows him.
The general is an alert, observer', and
picks up a vast deal of information on
his travels. He alights on many a fact
and incident which one not gifted with
rare power of discernment might pass
unnoticed. To-day he was the "first in
the party to discover that Riverside is
a prohibition city.
Early in the evening a train from
San Bernardino arrived bringing two
hundred active Republicans. Other
specials and the regular trains rein
forced the local ranks. As the hour of
7 o'clock drew near the people wended
their way to the Loririg Opera-house.
Shortly after the doors were thrown
open the spacious house was crowded.-
All the seats on the stage, the boxes
and the circles were occupied. The
standing room was. appropriated with-,
out delay.- On the street in front of the
Loring the Riverside City band played
patriotic and popular music. The en
thusiasm deepened as the throng in
creased. The people came from Colton,
Redlands, Ontario, Perrls, San Jacinto,
Elsinore, Corona and the country pre
cincts immediately surrounding River
side.
When Henry T. Gage;, accompanied
by General Barnes, J. C. Needham, I.
N. Blackstock, the nominee for Rail
way Commissioner, and Major Kyle
took their places on the stage the audi
ence gave three hearty cheers.
The meeting was called to . order by
C. F. Huse, chairman of the county
committee. The first feature of the ex
ercises was a song by the Republican
Silver-Tongued Quartet of colored
boys. The song, "Good Evening, Mr.
Gage. Arn't You Not Glad That You
A:e Living and Quite Well?" made a
decided hit and the singers â Fred
White, Will Blanton, Tom Wilson and
Peter Polkâ were recalled. They sang
"Maguire Is a Foolish Man," with a
refrain, "Send Those Angels Down."
Captain M. J. Daniels was announced
as the chairman of the meeting. He
named the following vice-presidents:
San Bernardino â James Hiitchings,
Judge John L. Campbell, C. C. Has
kell, G. T. Copeland, James Fleming, T.
J. Fording, F. W. Richardson; Red
landsâH. H. Sinclair, R. C. Avery

In a speech, able and well timed,
Captain Daniels predicted Republican
triumph.
Hon. J. C. Needham was presented
to the audience as the first speaker of
the evening. The Congressional nomi
nee made a eood speech. Hia exorea
sions and his bearing won the favor of
his hearers.
When Mr. Gage, the standard-bear-r,
was presented the applause was mighty
and prolonged. He was introduced as
a man belonging to' the people.
Mr. Gage did not speak at great
length, as he was constrained by the
courtesies of the campaign to yt -Id
some of his time to the "Congressionui
nominee, who was advertised to- speak
here to-night. Many of the questions

The single tax heresies advocated by
Maguire were review*** The people of
Riverside were thankd for the support

speaker in the nomin^ fi ng convention
at Sacramento.. Attention was Called
to Maguire's assault on the integrity
.and manhood of ' the Riverside dele
gates when he spoke of them as rail

Sacramento- by corruption. Said Mr.

"1 would not assail the Democratic
convention 'which nominated Mr. Ma
guire. I say it is beneath the dignity
and .honor of any one seeking the high

brand his fellow-man."
The speech of Mr. Gage was more in
the nature of a frank and confidential
talk with his neighbors than a formal
address. He replied happily to the criti
cism concerning his clothes and his
habit of putting his hands into his own
pockets. He advised his critics to de

saw the suit he would wear at the in-
augural ball.
Maguire's war record was reviewed.
The records were quoted to show that
Maguire voted against the war revenue
measure and did not vote to allow the
gallant soldiers the right of suffrage in
the Held. Maguire's record on the rail
road settlement bill was presented to
the audience. They paid close atten

gressional Record. This expose of Ma
guire's position on the railroad settle
ment created a sensation here as it did
in San Diego, San Bernardino and
Santa Ana. Great applause folLowed
the exposure of Maguire's attempt to
blend the judgment of the people in
this matter. The attitude of Senator
Morgan and Senator White toward
what Maguire called an infamous bill
was cited.
In closing, Mr. Gage promised that if
elected Governor he would give the
people of California a clean, honest and
business-like administration. â  The si
lence of the audience as Mr. Gage made
this pledge was solemn and impressive.
When he concluded the sentence there
was a- burst of applause. The audience
was impressed by his candor and sin
cerity.
The Silver Tongued Quartet sang
another song, "Good-by, Maguire,
Good-by." General Barnes was pre
sented to the vast assemblage as Cali
fornia's br'Hiant orator. The general
got great rounds of applause as he

In masterly and eloquent fashion the
brilliant orator reviewed the platforms
of the parties and paid his respects to
the fusion nominee for Governor. He
quoted Maguire's language exalting the
nihilists as gentlemen holding uni
versity degrees. Reference was made
to Maguire's constant harping on the

proposition to build a lighthouse, Ma
guire introduced the single tax. The
inference was drawn that the "Little
Giant" and his followers would dwell
constantly on single tax if fusion

"If Maguire should be elected- and
after his .election die, oh, heavens and
earth! Hutchinson Governor of this

The roars of laughter responsive to

I Republicans of Riverside entertain in

The fusion of .political elements in
the cause of Magruireism was t pro
j nouniced "an unholy alliance of office
hunters." Silver Republicans were
implored to return to the party of
Lincoln, Grant, Hayes, Harrison and
McKinley. The mention of McKinley's
name was always the signal for great

dress. He appreciated the refinement
and intelligence of the Riverside au
dience. He closed his masterly speech
with a tribute to the prosperity, pa

Early to-morrow morning Mr. Gage

PITCHED BATTLE
IN THE STREETS
Striking Miners Fight
Negroes.
PENA, ILL, IS TIE SCENE
SEVERAL HUNDRED SHOTS ARH
EXCHANGED.
Business Suspended in the Town and
an Appeal Made to Governor
Tannar to Send < c
Troops.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PANA. 111., Sept. 28.â Striking union
coal miners and imported negroes en
gaged in a pitched battle in the main
street of this town to-night. Several
hundred shots were exchanged, no one
was wounded in the ranks of the union,
men. The negroes were driven from
the city to their stockades, carrying
with them, it is believed, a number of
wounded comrades. One of the negroes
is reported to have died soon after
reaching the stockades.
Desultory firing continues at mid*
night in the vicinity of the stockades.
The trouble was precipitated at 8:30
o'clock this evening. As usual, the
negroes from the stockades at the
Springside and Penwell mines were
making demonstrations on Second and.
Locust streets, the principal business
streets of the city, by parading heavily
armed. The union miners were in ses
sion at their hall, where a Chicago
labor leader was speaking.
One of the negroes appeared at the
foot of the miners' hall and engaged In
a quarrel with a union white miner.
Officer Samuel Smith immediately ar
rested the black man and was escortinsr
him to jail, when he was closed in on
by a posse of negroes, who, pointing
their revolvers at Smith, threatened to
kill him if he did not release his pris
oner. Smith continued on his way to
the jail with his prisoner. Union min
ers and others meanwhile went to
Smith's assistance and the negroes
Wf-n driven back. Smith took his
prisoner to Operator George V. Pen
well's residence, and upon Penwell's
standing fur the negro's fine he was
released.
Before Smith had released his pris
oner, however, the negro posse had
been reinforced and assumed a threat
ening attitude toward the white men.
David MeGavic, leader of the union
miners, clubbed one of the blacks on
| the head ' ith a revolver, it is said. For
: half a block MeGavic forced the ne
groes to retreat, and then a few shots
were fired. The negroes retreated
â double quick to their stockades, se
cured . rifles, returned to Locust street
and' challenged the miners to a fight.
The opposing forces lined up on the
street, the negroes with Winchesters
and the miners with shotguns, rifles
I and revolvers. Neighboring business
! houses were immediately closed, lights
extinguished and citizens generally
sought their homes. At the word of
command firing commenced. The first
j volley, it is said, came from the ne-
I groes. The union men responded wim
a volley, and the heavy firing continued
for five minutes. Much of the shooting
\ was wild and entirely harmless to the
j white men, who finally drove the
enemy in full retreat to the stockades.
A second encounter between whites
i and blacks occurred at the mines after
I the first battle near Penwell stockade,
! but the firing was scattered, and it is
not believed to have been a serious
engagement. The miners had full
charge of the business streets at mid
night. Desultory rifle reports could be
I heard from the Penwell and Springside
stockades, but no person would ven
ture into the streets near the mines,
and very few are loitering about the
business or residence sections.
The union miners say the battle to
night is only a foretaste of what may
be expected to follow.
Governor Tanner will be asked to
send the militia to protect property in
this city and remove the negroes.

GOVERNOR SHOT
BY AN INMATE
Attempt to Murder Colonel (3r>
drew J. Smith at Santa
MoQica.
Five Bullets fired Into His Body by a War
Veterao Who fancied t\e Had a
Grievance.
LOS ANGELES, Sept 2S.â Andrew J.
Smith, governor of the Pacific branch
of the National Home for disabled sol
diers, lies at his home at the point of
death with five bullets from the revol
ver of a would-be assassin in his body.
He may live, but three of the bullet
wounds are serious. He is of advanced
age, but he has a strong physique.
The assassin who attempted to take
the life of Governor Smith Is Albert G.
Bradley, a native of Vermont, aged 57.
He served during the "War of the Re
bellion In the Second Vermont Infan
try. He was admitted to the home
first in 1593; was discharged In 1596, and
readmitted in 1897. He has also twice
been an inmate of the State Home for
disabled veterans, located at Yount
ville. There is no question, at least
among those who know him best, aÂ»
to Bradley's sanity. He was a pecu
liarly moody person and had a very
vicious temper, of which he gave fre
quent exhibitions among his comrades.
At &:45 this morning Governor Smith
had just completed his daily Inspec
tion of the quarters and grounds of the
home. Alter he left the guardhouse
he started for the headquarters build
ing to sign some papers. Just as he
wus about to enter the buiidnig from
the rear, Bradley, â who evidently had
lying In ambush for him, passed
bmith ana stepped to one side. As he
did so he drew his revolver and opened
lire upon Governor Smith.
As rapidly as he could he emptied
the five chambers of a 38-caJiber re
volver into Governor Smith's body.
The first two shots were fired almost
together; then there was an interval 'ot
several seconds and the remaining
three shuts -.Â»Â»jre fired.
When the shots were fired the vic
tim was about thirty feet from the
steps of the headquarters building. He
did not fall, but walked on. Major
W. D. Graham and Orderly Pottle at
the Bound of the first shot ran to the
back porch. Graham carried Governor
Smith inside and Pottle secured the
murderer, who was taken to the guard
house.
The home surgeons made an exami
nation and found that one bullet had
passed through Smith's right arm be
tween the ahuuider and elbow; another
d through the right thigh and
came QUt at the groin; a. third entered
the back Just below the shoulder blade.
Two bullets have not been located.
Governor Smith suiTered much pain
during the .entire day and to-night his
]!:'â¢â  hangs in the balance.
As soon as the intelligence of Brad
ley'a awful deed became noised about
the home, the veterans manifested In
tense feeling. Bradley was not popu
lar with any of them. He was always
very bitter against those In authority
and had anarchistic views. After he
had been lodged in the guardhouse
threats of lynching were heard on all
sides. A Santa Monica officer was
called and Bradley was brought to the
County Jail in this city for safe keep-
Ing.
He was seen this evening by The
Call correspondent. He seemed, to re
alize fully the consequences of his act
and appeared to be somewhat repent
ant. All he would say was that he
must have been crazy when he fired
the shots and that he was sorry.
Just after he had unloaded his revol
ver into his victim he talked differently.
He said then that he was sorry he had
not killed him, for that was what he
intended to do.
Bradley's victim was a brigadier gen
eral of volunteers during the civil war.
"When Morgan's threatened raid fright
ened all Northern Ohio General Smith
and his New York brigade were sta
tioned at Johnsons Island, off the city
of Sandusky, to prevent possible release
of Confederate prisoners of war con
fined there. In 1867 he became connected
with National Soldiers' Homes, first
being stationed at the Maine Home. He
was transferred later to Leavenworth.
About two years ago he was placed in
charge here.
Governor Smith had trouble at
Leavonworth with a few inmates,
which was dignified by a Congressional
investigation owing to the bitter op
position and personal ill feeling of
Congressmen R. W. Blue and Case
Broderick of Kansas. He was exon
erated.
Last November The. Call correspond
ent devoted two days to an investiga
tion of the complaints made by some
twenty-five of the 2200 inmates of the
home at Santa Monica. About fifty
veterans were interviewed at random.
Only two had any complaints to make
and they were of the most trivial char
acter. Governor Smith was then seen
and he gave the correspondent carte
blanche to inspect or examine any de*
partment Of the home or ; to interview
any and all inmates if he desired. He
paid that he had issued orders against
pictures bÂ»ing put on the waLls of the
quarters as men sometimes placed vul
gar and obscene photographs and litho
graphs on the walls. He had also re
quir.-tj of the inmates that they at all
tinr-s keep their quarters clean and
neat.
Absolutely nothing could be found
upon which to base a reasonable charge
against Governor Smith. But a Los
Angeles paper opened up Its columns to'
the "kicking" inmates and printed.col
umns <>f matter written by the inmates
about Governor Smith's management,
claiming It wae tyrannical and that he
used autocratic powers. Thers was no
verification of these statements and al
most to a man the inmates exonerated
the governor. Yet the paper referred
to continued to print diatribes against
Smith, giving publicity in that line to
all tiiat was Bent in to it. Some few
veterans finally believed they did have
a grievance and were very bitter
toward Smith. The result was the at
tempted assassination to-day. Smith
did not know Bradtey and had never
reprimanded him. Bradley had never
been in the guardhouse.
Smith will not die to-night. His
greatest danger is from blood poison
ing.
GOVERNOR SMITH'S
PREVIOUS TROUBLES
" LEAVENWORTH, Kas., Sept. 28.â
Governor Smith was in charge of the
National Home at Leavenworth for
many years. Early on the morning of
June 11, 1897, his residence on tho
home groiinds at Leavenworth was
wrecked by dynamite. The Governor,
bis wlfo and daughter, were In the
house, but escaped with minor Injuries.
The crime was traced to a veteran, Jo
seph W. Oliver, a member of the home,
and he was convicted.
.Governor Smith's administration at
the Leavenworth hc-me was marked by
many troubles. Through the efforts of
Richard W. Blue, then a member of
Congress from Kansas, the management
of the home was investigated by
a committee from the House of Rep
resentatives. Many charges were pre
ferred, against Governor Smith, but the
investigation resulted in no more than
his transfer to the. governorship of the
Pacific branch, where he was wounded
to-day.
The charges acrainst Governor Smith
that led to the investigation were prin
cipally mistreatment of the old soldiers.
It was said that he confined riien in the
insane wards of the hospital without
previous, examination by the surgeon;
that he maintained a police system
cruel in the extreme, and that he forced
men to taite the gold treatment for
drunkenness against their will: Colonel
Sidney G. Cooke of Herrington, . Kas.,
the local manager, testified that he had
ordered Governor Smith to discharge
brutal police-officers and to cease the
system of forcing the soldiers to take
the cure for drunkenness. The order,
lw said, had been disobeyed. Later
Colonel Cooke stated that he had in
vestigated some complaints made by
pensioners that their pensions had been
withheld from them by order of the
Governor and against the law. He had
ordered this to cease and had been
obeyed. An investigation of this had
also been made by ex-Governor Glick,
Pension Commissioner. Governor Smith
had been let off with a reprimand. Fur
ther charges against the governing of
the Soldiers' Home were made that he
had abused and insulted officers und?r
him, had caused men carried on Gov
ernment dÂ«v rolls to do chores about,
his house and had turned Government
property aver to his. own private use.
Before becoming governor of the
Leavenworth Home. Smith was for
three years treasurer of the National
Soldiers' Home at To-gas, Me. Charges
preferred against him th*Â»re by the gov
ernor of that home led to his transfer
in ISSS to Leavenworth.

CAUSES THREE DEATHS
Disaster at a Camp Occupied by La-
borers Near New What
com, Wash.
NEW WHATCOM, Wash., Sept. 28.â 8y
an explosion of what Is thought to have
been dynamite at Gee's Camp, near Fern
dale, this county, three jnen lost their
lives and two more will die. The dead
are:
MIKE GEE JR.
â  SWANSON, a small son of the
cook.
Fatally injured: Dan Lanstrom and the

These are the ln.tf?st advices to the Seat
tle Times' correspondent here and are au
thentic.
The explosion occurred seventeen miles
from telegraphic communication and no
further news will reach here before morn-
Ing. The scene of. the explosion is about a
half mile from Custer, a small. Inland
town on the line of the Great Northern
Railroad.
The camp is the home of the men en
gaged in the clearing of a right-of-way
for the plank road between this city and
Blame. The explosion is thought to have
been caused by dynamite being placed in
the oven to dry and which became over
heated, resulting in the terrible disaster.
