THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, THTJHSDAY, SEPTEMBER 29, 1898.
FOLLOWED HER
LOVER TO THE
FROZEN NORTH
Mad Infatuation of a Married
Woman for a Kotzebue
Gold Hunter.
Mrs. Hermann Stowed Herself Away on the
Northern Light and Is Now a Cook
for Her Sweetheart.
The bark Northern Light got hack froro
Kctzi.-bue Bound yesterday. Sne is thj
first cf Lho fleet to return that left hero
for the gold fields on the Kubucl: River
five months ago. and tho people who
came bnck on her bring all kinds of
news. There are tales of disaster ond
tragedy, of Masted hopes and good for
tune; a love story with a problematical
ending, ana many sudden deaths. Of iho
1500 people who rushed to Kotzebue in
search of a fortune twenty-nine are sa'.d
to have lost their lives in the treacher
ous waters of the Kubuck River. Strange
to tay, not one of the men who came
down on the Northern Light can tell
the names of those who perished. All
they can tell is that a launch was sunk
in such and such a place; a landslide
covered a boat at another point on the
river, while yet another launch struck
a snag and went down with all hands.
Thoso who came back on the Northern
Light were: A. O. Liudsey, C. E. Gor
don. G. W. Pickett, J. G. Williams,
Charles Gleaves, C. T. Le Mare, Dr. E.
A. Robinson, H. Mather, \V. Scott, J.
Blumer, J. P. Larkin, T. Johnston, C. A.
Haehl, C. E. Watson, W. L. Rockwell,
A. L. Vincent, E. S. Guiterrez, L. E.
Easton, W. W. Gurgles, Captain W. F.
Johnston of the schooner Louisa D, L.
W. Johnston and Walter Johnston. Of
these L>r. Robinson and T. Johnston went
up on The Northern Light. They did not
like the prospects and returned. The
other passengers (148 all told) decided to
remain and try their luck. Mate Tilton
of the Northern Light asserts that not
more than seventy-rive gold hunters out
of the 15U0 who went up will return with
the fleet. Color was struck in a num
j ber of places, but nothing great. While
I tho Haynes party from Los Angeles were
; awaiting the arrival of their provisions
they bored down into the soil, and at the
lirst attempt brought up a nugget.
However, the love story comes first.
Charles W. Baumann was formerly a
member of the Pacific Coast Co-operative
Cigar Manufacturing Company. Whfle
living at the New Washington "Hotel he
formed the acquaintance of a Mrs. Her
mann, a handsome young married woman.
She iel! madly In love with "Charley,"
and finally made his life miserable with
her attentions. In order to escape her he
sold out his interest in the cigar fac
tory and quietly took passage on the bark
Alaska for Kotzebue. Everything was
kept very quiet, and Charley got away
without Mrs. Hermann knowing any
thing about it.
A few days after the Alaska had sailei
Mrs. Hermann discovered the flight of
her lover. She went to Captain White
side of the Northern Light and with
tears in her eyes told of her husband
having deserted her and their little child
in order to reach the new El Dorado.
She told of the sale of his business and
flight on the Alaska, and begged for a
passage to Kotzebue. Captain White
side made some inquiries on the outside
and refused the lovesick lady a passage.
Ho was not to get rid of her so easily,
however.
Three days after the Northern Light
got to sea it was reported to the captain
that the vessel was leaking. The pumps
were sounded, and Capain Whlteside
found the bark was making water fast.
The story of the three auger holes in the
bow of the ship and their location by
Mate Tilton has already been told exclu
sively in The Call, but his other find has
not yet been made public.
Stowed away in a snug little nest among
the cargo was Mrs. Hermann. She was
well supplied with food and water but
nearly dead from seasickness and her
cramped position. She told Captain
Whiteside when brought on deck that she
would kill herself if she was not taken
along. Once when Baumann tried to dis
card her she nearly killed him. so she
said, and twice she attempted suicide be
cause he would not love her with all his
heart and soul. Life held no pleasure for
her apart from Charley, so whether he
WELCH HAS NO USE
FOR THE CUBANS
THE OFFICER TELLS OF HIS EX
PERIENCE AT SANTIAGO.
Impressions of a California Soldier
' Narrated to the Ladies of the
Forum Club.
The Forum Club gave another charm
ing reception and entertainment yester
day in Sherman & clay Hall on Sutter
street, Mrs. F. L. Whitney presiding.
Mrs. J. P. Young received the members
and guests as they arrived. After the
regular business of the organization was
transacted Sidney Drew, the actor, read
an interesting paper upon his experience
at a benefit in a small theater in the West
End of London. He also recited a couple
of short poems, which were well appre
ciated. A cornet solo by Louis Harrison
received much applause. An interesting
address was given by Samuel Shortridge
on "The Late War With Spain,"in which
he advocated the holding of the Philip
pine Islands. Just before refreshments
were announced Lieutenant L. M. Welch
formerly of the Twenty-fourth United
States Infantry but now of the Twen
tieth, told of his experience before Santi
ago, Cuba.
Lieutenant Welch is the son of the well
known Dr. Welch of this city. The youne
man is a graduate of West Point, and at
the breaking out of the war he was with
his regiment at Fort Worth. Utah In
his address tho young soldier made no at
tempt at oratory, but told In an unaffect
ed manner of what he had seen and
heard His style of narration quickly
found favor with his audience. At times
the humorous side of army scenes was
presented in a way that shows that a sol
dier can appreciate fun as well as light
ing. His address took the form of a run
ning narrative of what happened from
the time his regiment broke camp to the
time that he was laid low on the battle
field of Santiago with that dread disease
yellow fever, which made his return to
civilization necessary. Of the movements
of the army, the quickness and dispatch
in doing things the lieutenant spoke in
the highest praise.
The reception of the soldiers at Tampa
Florida, ho Raid was nothing like that
given to those who came to San Fran
cisco. Here they were well cared for by
the citizens, but there they were regarded
as a legitimate prey. Prices were doubled
for all articles of trade, and it was not
long before shop-keepers who had pre
viously been barely making a living were
rolling in money and overbearing in man
ner.
For the Cubans proper Lieutenant
Welch has but little respect. They seem
to have but two ambitionsâ to steal and
eat, and to avoid work as though it was
another form of 'yellow jack."
In contrast to his opinion of the na
tives of Cuba the young officer has thr
highest respect for the army mule, which
is a hero, carrying ammunition and food
into the very heart of the fighting, liv
ing on what he could get, and never com
plaining. The conduct of the Spanish
sharpshooters j n disregarding all rules of
civilized warfare In shooting wounded
soldiers and Red Cross nurses was de
nounced by the American. It was a mat
ter of the deepest regret of Lieutenant
Welch that he should haveÂ»been attacked
liked ft or not Captain Whiteside had to
ta^e her along.
Now, the Alaska, although she left
before the Northern Light, had to step
at St. Lawrence Island -to build a river
boat. So the Northern Light was the
first to reach Kotzebue Sound. When
Charley stepped ashore, therefore, at the
new El Dorado, Mrs. Hermann was there
to greet him. Absence must have made
the heart grow fonder, for when the
Northern Light left the Sound twenty
eight days ago Mrs. Hermann had been
installed as cook of Charley's camp, and
â ;ill went merry as a marriage bell."
Mrs. Hermann's husband and child are
still in San Francisco.
Mate Tilton of the Northern Light is
the man who performed the marvelous
feat of walking across Alaska in the dead
of winter with news from the imprisoned
whalers at Point Barrow. In talking
about the Kotzeuue Sound rush he said:
"Nearly all the people who went up
there will remain all winter. About
seventy-five men have got cold feet and
will come back wth taÂ»es of hard luck,
but I believe that many of those who
have remained behind will strike it rich.
The Haynes and Lane parties were satis
fied with the prospects. John Tallant of
the Tallant Banking Company and a
party of six went up the Buckland River
and I think will do well. Mr. Tallant was
in good health.
"A boat from the bark Mermaid, con
taining six men, capsized, and the men
were lost. -. search party was sent out,
but all that they recovered was the boat
and some of the men's clothing. Two
men from the steamer Grace Dollar were
making their way up the Kubuck River
in a boat when a landslide came down
anu covered the small craft. One oÂ£ the
men escaped by jumping overboard, but
the other was buried. One launch was
run ashore and wrecked, another sank,
and her boilers were pulled out in trying
to raise her. Three or four were swamped
owir.g to overloading, and !n every in
stance the loss of the boat was attended
with the loss of life. Captain Weeks got
tlrrd of tne Mermaid venture and is com
ing down on the Jane A. Falkenberg, and
Captain Johnson of the Louisa D also
'reneged' on the game and came back
"The Jane A. Falkenberg, Catherine Sud
den, Mermaid, Alaska and Louisa D were
all at Kotzebue when .we left, but were
gt-tting ready for a start. Captain Cogan
was to go whaling in tho Alaska and will
not get home until November. The Mer
maid was to go to St. Michael and the
other vessels are coming to Pan Fran
cisco. The natives who came to San Fran
cisco from Point Barrow with, me are on
tnelr way up the Kubuck River with a
party sent out by the Liebes. Tiki was
born and raised in the Kotzebue country
and should be valuable to his party.
"On our way down we ran into a ter
rific storm, and the vessel was hove to un
der a 'goose winged' lower maintop sail
Z r se i* oral days. Many of the passen^r.s
thought their last day had come, but the
out old ship never took a pint of water
Peter Laflin, purser of the Northern
Light, was left behind at Kotzebue

private business, and a fair wind spring
ing up in the meantime the captain nut

If ,}\? deha were not so Pretty she

that her father refused to allow her
because he wanted her to peddle his

She does not want to be married she
says, and if she did she is sure her
father would allow her to do so, and I
her father never asked her to peddle
his wares. He is very good to her she

RAILROAD COMMISSION.
Pullman Matters Laid Over and
Communications on Hand.
At the meeting of the Railroad Com
missioners yesterday afternoon the in
quiry into the affairs of the Pullman
Company with a view to enforce a re
duction of charges was postponed for two
weeks. The Commissioners are fortified
in their determination to press an inves
tigation by the number of communica
tions which they have received from par
ties in and out of the State,. The follow
ing Is a letter from Boston, and is a fair
representative of all the communications
received:
BOSTON. Mars., Sept. 16, 1898
To the State Board of Railroad Commission
ers of California: Inclosed clipping taken this
morning from a Boston paper. When a com
pany with a surplus nearly as large as their
capital, making big money every year, paying
fat dividends and adding to their surplus every
year also, employ the.r porters at \\Z to $25 per
month with the understanding, tacit IT not im
plied, that the public must tip them or not be
served, I think It Is time to coll a holt
Am glad you have taken this matter up, and
trust you will press it to a successful termina
tion. I wish every State in the Union would
follow suit.
I am not a socialist or anarchist, only a na
tive Bostonian, but I am weary of being among
those who are imposed upon by corporations
Yours very respectfully. D. W. KIMBALL,,

Camp and Crescent City and points be
tween. The former rate was 50 cents

City and Smith River RaJJroad operates

FORGETFULNESS
Mrs. Margaret Shields Makes a Bungling At

t HALF of ten cents' worth of
l\ whisky" was the only com-
J~\ fort, the sole consolation, Mrs.
â *â  â¢*â¢ Margaret Shields, the bungling
Incendiary, longed for yester
day. Her head ached, her body was
worn and tired and she suffered all
the pangs and physical tortures
that alcohol alone could assuage. A
small tumbler of- the coveted spirits
swallowed straight, surely and
steadily, with every evidence of
gratification, and the nerve-racked
woman lay back on her narrow
white cot to woo the sleep that had
persistently held aloof for nearly
ninety hours.
At 4 yesterday morning ;he fire
queen made a clumsy attempt tv
reach the shores of the "undiscov
ered country" by means of a hand
towel, which she had fastened firm
ly round her neck. The heavy
breathing of the would-be suicide
woke her cellmate. Mrs. Cordelia
Botkin, and she. with the matron,
Mrs. Casey, went to the sufferer's
assistance. Mrs. Shields was quickly
restored to this sphere of woe and
suffering and transferred to the Re
ceiving Hospital, where she rested
all day yesterday.
There is nothing attractive nor
prepossessing about the woman who
calmly admits that she committed
arson "to get moneyâ the insurance
of course." The seriousness of the
charge against her, the awful crin.e
of which she is accused, leave her
cold, hard and indifferent as to the
grave punishment that raav ensue.
She appears to be thoroughly ir
responsible. Beacom lit the match
and, according to his accomplice,
Beacom is the guilty one. the man
to whom the rigid penalties for
arson are to be meted out.
In discussing her terrible crime,
Mrs bhields makes no attempt at
concealment. The facts, as she re
calls them, do not. impress her with
the full measure of their hideous
ness. "Beacom made a botch of it,"
she says and therein to the pris
oner lies the only crime.
She has no tears, no bewailings no
vain regrets. The thought of the
suffering of her children brings the
hard lines round her large coarse
mouth and her gray eyes snap de
fiance, as she mumblingly reiter
ates; I don't care about my chil
dren. They're old enough; let them
look out for themselves; they say
they will never see me again. May
be they are right. I don't care!"
It required no persuasion, no dip
lomatically administered coaxing to
induce Mrs. Shields to give her
crime in detail for publication
From Sunday night until Tuesday
evening, when she voluntarily sur
rendered herself, the woman wan
dered about the streets, and the
constant strain left her mind fagged
out. though her strong body was
comparatively fresh and rested It
was with difficulty that she kept her
attention to the point of her story
and the details of her offense were
Interpolated with groans of anguish
as she complainde about the terri
ble pain in her head.
'7, di^? ft t0 Set the insurance mon
ey, Mrs. Shields said. "Henry
Beacom and I had been talking it
over for more than seven months.
I he house was Insured for $2100, and
Beacom was to receive $100 and all
the furniture if he made a good job
of It. If not, he was only to get
the furniture. Bcacom was build
ing a house and he wanted my man
tel and folding doors very much for
his place; so he carried them away
"Two weeks before the fire wo
took Beacom's wife into our confi
dence. First she objected, but grad
ually we won her around, and on
Saturday night when we carried
out the furniture she lent us her
assistance. My. how we worked in
all that rain. Beacom gave us one
dollar, and we went and bought
some whisky to keep up our cour
age. Beacom took an ax and a
hammer to break off the mantel
shelf, and his wife handed him the
tools while I held the light. Lillie
Mullins knew all about the fire, but
she would not help us. She cried
and begged us not to do it.
THE CHINESE SLAVES.
Twenty-Three Remanded for the
Present to the Custody of
the Missions.
Of the twenty-six Chinese slaves ar
rested by Federal officers in the raid last
Tuesday three were released yesterday on
the presentation of the registration cer
tificates by their putative husbands. The
remaining twenty-three were taken before
United States District Judge de Haven
and identified. The Judge ordered that
pending their examination they should be
confined in the Presbyterian Chinese Mis
sion Home on Sacramento street and ihe
Methodist Mission Home on Washington
street.
The women were taken from the court
room to two express wagons in waiting
before the Sansome-street entrance to the
Appraiser's building, and Into these they
were loaded.
One of the women is a slave dealer and
will be prosecuted for that offense.

"Beacom got the cans of coal oil,

"I needed the insurance money to
make up for my losses at the race
track. I got to going on 'ladies'
free day,' lost money and went
again to try and get it back."
Mrs. Shields told her story with
many interruptions, in which she
piteously begged for "a half of 10
cents' worth of whisky" to put her
to sleep. "If I had the whisky I
know I could go to sleep," she
said. "The trouble with me is that
I am crazy, but I am not so crazy
that I don't know it."
Of her attempted suicide Mrs.
Shields declares that she remem
bers nothing-. "When I left my
home on Sunday night," .she ex
plained, "I went hunting for the
bay to drown myself. Finally I met
Mrs. McLnuerhlln" and asked -her
where the bay was, and she brought
me here to the prison."
On the promise of a potion of
whisky Mrs. Shields sat up in bed,
turned her â  face to the light and
let the artist make a sketch of her.
"All the ladies on the hill," she
said, "have had their pictures in
the paper." .
The coveted whisky was brought.
Mrs. Shields took it, sighed a deep,
contented sigh and tears gathered
in her stupid eyesâ the only tears
that moistened them during the
entire interview; tears of content
ment for the pleasure the whisky
afforded her.

ings stopped. -
"This is a matter for the trial
Judge and a jury to pass upon," he
said. "The woman is charged with
a felony and must ; stand trial. - It
is for a jury to decide whether she is
a responsible person."
That ended the - examination, and
Mrs. Shields was taken back to the
